(a) Schematic representation of S. mutans population history. The timeline (in years before present) represents the start of the expansion of cariogenic bacteria after the onset of agriculture, calibrated using an experimentally determined mutation rate for bacteria (Drake 1991), concomitant with an in vivo determined generation time for oral flora bacteria (Gibbons 1964) (see Materials and Methods and Supplementary Material online, for details). (b) The observed distribution of number of synonymous SNPs at a given frequency in the sample of 58 isolates (blue) is shown, as well as the expectation under the parameters that generate the best fit demographic model (dark blue). The difference between the two distributions is not significant. The distribution under a standard neutral model with constant population size is shown in light blue (significant KS, P &lt; 0.0001). (c) The bi-dimensional likelihood profile for combination of parameters ν (ratio of current to ancestral population size) in the x axis and the time at the beginning of the demographic expansion (scaled in generations/2Na) in the y axis. The maximum likelihood value is shown as a white dot and the 95% CI is highlighted as a white dotted line. 95% CI estimated from bootstrapped data can be found in supplementary figure S7, Supplementary Material online."}

<meta property="og:image:secure_url" content="https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/m_mss278f1p.jpeg?Expires=1766108132&amp;Signature=iPTmUu1rKz64dtvPrpv5A-0Brcb0DcOACIFnmorruS5I8ioVNg4HG0o7s8jV0yvGujRN7wGUtiIK0IVJfQiCpOlV~IJxqE6xYcmUq-TSoVIIzKI-BCCDdRKMwLeZShRPKyVTrVh6bBDZZHtJalYbTXZFwtN1NVrWR4A9Dl04b7DGdh5VnsFJTsYIq3EzP-jV1blwCqE82Sd0G715remn2BROGduKNNjJR-VmN7-dsjcguKoiGcwCnisl37eiG3fftG3m2eLhVAdtKBGPPdKPvj6OYT1Dpnq9mxCpTNIOCjX5OSV390f69xeSIcc~ZeIPvCpFYT2KgGHl9DiBsNOF2A__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA"/>
<meta property="og:image:alt" content="Demographic history of Streptococcus mutans. (a) Schematic representation of S. mutans population history. The timeline (in years before present) represents the start of the expansion of cariogenic bacteria after the onset of agriculture, calibrated using an experimentally determined mutation rate for bacteria (Drake 1991), concomitant with an in vivo determined generation time for oral flora bacteria (Gibbons 1964) (see Materials and Methods and Supplementary Material online, for details). (b) The observed distribution of number of synonymous SNPs at a given frequency in the sample of 58 isolates (blue) is shown, as well as the expectation under the parameters that generate the best fit demographic model (dark blue). The difference between the two distributions is not significant. The distribution under a standard neutral model with constant population size is shown in light blue (significant KS, P < 0.0001). (c) The bi-dimensional likelihood profile for combination of parameters ν (ratio of current to ancestral population size) in the x axis and the time at the beginning of the demographic expansion (scaled in generations/2Na) in the y axis. The maximum likelihood value is shown as a white dot and the 95% CI is highlighted as a white dotted line. 95% CI estimated from bootstrapped data can be found in supplementary figure S7, Supplementary Material online."/>

    $(document).ready(function () {
        $('.article-top-widget').on('click', '.ati-toggle-trigger', function () {
            $(this).find('.icon-general-add, .icon-minus').toggleClass('icon-minus icon-general-add');
            $(this).siblings('.ati-toggle-content').toggleClass('hide');
        });

// In Chrome, an anchor tag with target="_blank" and a "mailto:" href opens a new tab/window as well as the email client
        // I suspect this behavior will be corrected in the future
        // Remove the target="_blank"
        $('ul.wi-affiliationList').find('a[href^="mailto:"]').each(function () {
            $(this).removeAttr('target');
        });
    });
</script>

<h2 scrollto-destination="90601076" id="90601076" class="abstract-title js-splitscreen-abstract-title">Abstract</h2>
<section class="abstract"><p class="chapter-para"><em>Streptococcus mutans</em> is widely recognized as one of the key etiological agents of human dental caries. Despite its role in this important disease, our present knowledge of gene content variability across the species and its relationship to adaptation is minimal. Estimates of its demographic history are not available. In this study, we generated genome sequences of 57 <em>S. mutans</em> isolates, as well as representative strains of the most closely related species to <em>S. mutans (S. ratti, S. macaccae,</em> and <em>S. criceti)</em>, to identify the overall structure and potential adaptive features of the dispensable and core components of the genome. We also performed population genetic analyses on the core genome of the species aimed at understanding the demographic history, and impact of selection shaping its genetic variation. The maximum gene content divergence among strains was approximately 23%, with the majority of strains diverging by 5–15%. The core genome consisted of 1,490 genes and the pan-genome approximately 3,296. Maximum likelihood analysis of the synonymous site frequency spectrum (SFS) suggested that the <em>S. mutans</em> population started expanding exponentially approximately 10,000 years ago (95% confidence interval [CI]: 3,268–14,344 years ago), coincidental with the onset of human agriculture. Analysis of the replacement SFS indicated that a majority of these substitutions are under strong negative selection, and the remainder evolved neutrally. A set of 14 genes was identified as being under positive selection, most of which were involved in either sugar metabolism or acid tolerance. Analysis of the core genome suggested that among 73 genes present in all isolates of <em>S. mutans</em> but absent in other species of the mutans taxonomic group, the majority can be associated with metabolic processes that could have contributed to the successful adaptation of <em>S. mutans</em> to its new niche, the human mouth, and with the dietary changes that accompanied the origin of agriculture.</p></section>                    <div class="article-metadata-panel clearfix at-ArticleMetadata"></div>
<div class="kwd-group"><a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;Streptococcus mutans&quot;"><em>Streptococcus mutans</em></a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;demographic inference&quot;">demographic inference</a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="cavities">cavities</a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;bacterial evolution&quot;">bacterial evolution</a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;pan and core genome&quot;">pan and core genome</a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;infectious disease&quot;">infectious disease</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="90601078" id="90601078" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Introduction</h2>
<p class="chapter-para"><em>Streptococcus mutans</em> is known to be one of the most prevalent bacteria in human oral flora and is widely recognized as a key etiological agent of human dental caries (reviewed in <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B13"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B13" data-open="mss278-B13" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Burne 1998</a>). Evidence in support of this latter issue include the following key points: <em>S. mutans</em> is frequently isolated from caries lesions; it induces caries formation in animals fed a sucrose-rich diet; the species is highly acidogenic and aciduric (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B41"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B41" data-open="mss278-B41" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Hamada and Slade 1980</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B56"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B56" data-open="mss278-B56" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Loesche 1986</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B82"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B82" data-open="mss278-B82" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">van Houte 1994</a>); it can form an effective biofilm by producing surface antigens which promote adhesion to the tooth surface and other bacteria (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B41"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B41" data-open="mss278-B41" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Hamada and Slade 1980</a>); and it flourishes in cariogenic plaque because it is better able to grow and metabolize carbohydrate in a low pH environment (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B8"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B8" data-open="mss278-B8" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Bender, et al. 1986</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B7"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B7" data-open="mss278-B7" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Bender and Marquis 1987</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B60"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B60" data-open="mss278-B60" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Marquis 1990</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B6"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B6" data-open="mss278-B6" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Belli and Marquis 1991</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B4"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B4" data-open="mss278-B4" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Arthur et al. 2011</a>). Despite its recognized importance in this important human disease, there are very few publications using comparative genomics to gain insights on basic biology, evolutionary history and pathogenesis of this organism (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B2"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B2" data-open="mss278-B2" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Ajdic et al. 2002</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B61"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B61" data-open="mss278-B61" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Maruyama et al. 2009</a>) and there are but three genome sequences currently on GenBank. A number of studies have demonstrated substantial genetic heterogeneity across clinical isolates of <em>S. mutans</em> (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B84"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B84" data-open="mss278-B84" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Zhang et al. 2009</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B4"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B4" data-open="mss278-B4" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Arthur et al. 2011</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B16"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B16" data-open="mss278-B16" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Cheon et al. 2011</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B69"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B69" data-open="mss278-B69" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Phattarataratip et al. 2011</a>); however, our present knowledge of gene content variability across the species and its relationship to adaptation and virulence is minimal.</p><p class="chapter-para">One of the more significant recent discoveries in bacterial genomics is that bacteria species appear to be comprised of both a set of core and dispensable genes, with only the former present in all isolates of that species and with the sum of the two components forming the species pan-genome (or supra-genome). This concept was first introduced for <em>S. agalactiae</em> in 2005 (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B79"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B79" data-open="mss278-B79" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Tettelin et al. 2005</a>) and is now generally regarded as a principle common to most or all bacteria. Much speculation has centered on the origin, composition, and size of bacteria pan-genomes and whether they are finite or infinite (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B80"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B80" data-open="mss278-B80" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Tettelin et al. 2008</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B52"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B52" data-open="mss278-B52" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Lapierre and Gogarten 2009</a>). Recently, we examined the role of the core and dispensable genes in defining two sympatric and closely related species of <em>Campylobacter</em>—<em>C. jejuni</em> and <em>C. coli</em> (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B53"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B53" data-open="mss278-B53" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Lefebure et al. 2010</a>), and addressed whether their pan-genomes are finite or infinite. We demonstrated, through the analysis of 96 genome sequences, that their pan-genome is indeed finite and that there are unique and cohesive features to each of their genomes defining their genomic identity. The two species have a similar pan-genome size; however, <em>C. coli</em> has acquired a larger core genome and each species has evolved a number of species-specific core genes, possibly reflecting different adaptive strategies. Understanding the pan-genome components of any species of bacteria means that the core genome and the dispensable genome can be identified for the group in question. Comparisons made to the genomes of representative isolates of other closely related species, can then identify the unique core genome of the group—that is, the genes common to all isolates of that group, not present in its closest relatives. This set of unique core genes is of particular interest, because they are amongst the genes likely to define the essence of that group’s adaptive specifics.</p><p class="chapter-para">Demographic models inferred from genetic data have an important role in modern population genetic analysis. Because demographic processes affect the accumulation of variation along the entire genome, the analysis of comparative population genome sequence data offers the possibility to address questions about the demographic history of populations. Of particular interest are genome-wide single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) from multiple individuals of the same species representing many thousands of quasi-independent data points. Site frequency spectrum (SFS) methods for the analysis of such data have proven to be a powerful means of assessing demographic history and have recently been applied to questions involving a diversity of organisms (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B15"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B15" data-open="mss278-B15" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Caicedo et al. 2007</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B39"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B39" data-open="mss278-B39" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Gutenkunst et al. 2009</a>). Demographic analyses of bacterial species based on population genetic analysis of whole genomes, using the SFS, have yet to be published, although such methods should be entirely applicable if the necessary data were available.</p><p class="chapter-para">Most of the intraspecific evolutionary analyses of bacterial species have focused on understanding the phylogenetic relationships among bacterial isolates or have concentrated on assessments of population diversity based on seven gene multilocus sequence typing (MLST). Studies based on MLST suggest that recombination in <em>S. mutans</em> contributes eight times more to the maintenance of genetic diversity than mutation (r/u ∼8.3) (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B23"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B23" data-open="mss278-B23" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Do et al. 2010</a>). Homologous recombination in <em>S. mutans</em>, as in the rest of naturally transformable species, occurs by a process analogous to gene conversion in eukaryotes, by replacement of short fragments of DNA (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B75"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B75" data-open="mss278-B75" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Smith et al. 1991</a>). Members of the genus <em>Streptococcus</em> have recently been the subject of population genomic analyses (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B25"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B25" data-open="mss278-B25" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Donati et al. 2010</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B19"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B19" data-open="mss278-B19" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Croucher et al. 2011</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B64"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B64" data-open="mss278-B64" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Muzzi and Donati 2011</a>), highlighting the importance of the dispensable gene component, which carries a high proportion of loci associated with different virulence and phenotypic attributes (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B77"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B77" data-open="mss278-B77" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Suzuki et al. 2011</a>). These studies have also pointed out the large impact of homologous recombination in the maintenance of genetic diversity of the core genome (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B25"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B25" data-open="mss278-B25" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Donati et al. 2010</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B19"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B19" data-open="mss278-B19" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Croucher et al. 2011</a>). Given the relatively high rates of homologous recombination in naturally transformable species like <em>S. mutans</em>, we propose employing methods based on the analysis of the SFS to provide insight on the evolutionary history of the species.</p><p class="chapter-para">In this study, we generated genome sequences of 57 <em>S. mutans</em> isolates, as well as representative strains of the most closely related species to <em>S. mutans</em>, to identify the overall structure and potential adaptive features of the dispensable and core components of the genome, and performed population genetic analyses on the core genome of the species aimed at understanding the demographic history, and impact of selection shaping its genetic variation.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="90601084" id="90601084" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Results</h2>
                    <h3 scrollto-destination="90601085" id="90601085" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">SNPs and the Core Genome</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">Next generation technology was used to obtain genome sequences of an international collection of 57 clinical isolates of <em>S. mutans</em>. Comparison of gene content among strains indicated that the maximum gene content divergence was approximately 23%, with many strains diverging by 5–15% in number of annotated genes (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S1</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online). On average, each genome contained 1,636 genes. To conduct a population genomic analysis of demographic history in <em>S. mutans</em><em>,</em> we needed to identify the core genome components because the necessary genetic information for reconstructing the demographic history of <em>S. mutans</em> is contained in those genes that are shared by all isolates of the species. Our comparisons indicated that there were 1,490 genes common to all 57 strains (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S2</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online, for an estimate of the core genome of <em>S. mutans</em>), out of which 1,430 had sufficient information (more than 90% of the gene length for all strains), to perform population genetic analyses; the pan-genome of the species approached 3,296 genes (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S3</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online). From the 1,430 core genes, we identified 29,805 silent and 21,997 replacement SNPs, which summarized across genes, resulted in estimates of sequence polymorphism ranging from 0.5% to 1.9%. We estimated summary statistics and found that the average GC content in <em>S. mutans</em> is approximately 37%, the average Watterson’s <em>θ</em> (0.008) is larger than the average nucleotide pairwise difference (0.005), and this translates into a prevalence of negative Tajima’s D across genes, as has been observed in other species of bacteria (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B45"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B45" data-open="mss278-B45" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Hughes 2005</a>). A graphic representation with the estimates of these summary statistics is included in the <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S4</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online). Only 1.16% of the SNPs found across all 1,430 core genes used in the analysis were multi-allelic leaving us with 98.84% bi-allelic SNPs for the demographic and selection analysis. Principal component analysis (PCA) (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B65"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B65" data-open="mss278-B65" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Novembre and Stephens 2008</a>), employing silent sites from the core genome, was used to inspect the structure of genetic variation. For this, we only considered synonymous polymorphic sites with minor allele frequencies above 5% and <em>r</em><sup>2 </sup>≤ 0.4; the correlation between alleles was used as a measure of linkage disequilibrium. The rationale behind the elimination of sites is that alleles in low frequency do not contribute to the clustering of strains and because the information in linked sites is redundant. Nevertheless, PCA performed with the complete data set (all synonymous polymorphic sites from the core genome) produced similar results. Consistent with the findings of other studies on <em>S. mutans</em> (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B23"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B23" data-open="mss278-B23" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Do et al. 2010</a>), our analysis suggested little genetic differentiation among isolates sampled in different geographic locations (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S5</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online). In addition to the PCA, we performed pairwise Fst analyses with permutation procedures to assess their significance. None of the pairwise Fst between geographic locations was significant. This facilitates the work of historical demographic reconstruction because single population models can be explored and fit to the data with greater power, because there are fewer numbers of parameters. Additional analyses on the dispensable part of the genome suggest that there is no association between the sharing of genes and the geographic location of the samples and like the core genome, there is no indication of population genetic structure evident in the analysis of high frequency dispensable genes.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="90601087" id="90601087" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Demographic History</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">To reconstruct the demographic history of <em>S. mutans</em>, we employed a maximum likelihood inference method based on the distribution of allele frequencies across silent SNPs, or SFS, and estimated confidence intervals (CIs) by bootstrapping. Five different population models were explored (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S6</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online) in this framework and the selection of the best-fit model was performed using the Akaike Information Criteria. The large number of singleton (unique) substitutions observed in <em>S. mutans</em> SFS is consistent with a recent expansion (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-F1"></span><a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="mss278-F1" class="link xref-fig">fig. 1</a><em>a</em> and <em>b</em>). Recently expanded populations leave a signature of mutations found in very low frequency, that have not had chance to disappear, or increase in frequency, by genetic drift (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B74"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B74" data-open="mss278-B74" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Slatkin and Hudson 1991</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B38"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B38" data-open="mss278-B38" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Griffiths and Tavare 1994</a>). Estimations performed with <em>δ</em>a<em>δ</em>i recover two main parameters under the exponential growth model. The first is <em>ν</em>, the ratio of current to ancestral population size; and the second is <em>τ</em>, the time since the change in demographic, representing in our case the start of the demographic expansion. <em>τ</em> is equal to time (<em>T</em>) scaled by 2Na, which is the ancestral effective population size. After appropriate rescaling of the inferred parameters, the maximum likelihood analysis suggested that the SFS of <em>S. mutans</em> is consistent with a demographic scenario in which the population started expanding exponentially approximately 10,000 years ago (95% CI<sub>classic_boostrap</sub>: 3,268–14,344 years ago; possible uncertainties in mutation rate and generation time were taken into consideration in the computation of this CI—see <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online for details; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-F1"></span><a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="mss278-F1" class="link xref-fig">fig. 1</a><em>a</em> and <em>b</em> and <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-T1"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-T1" data-open="mss278-T1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">table 1</a>) and the absolute fit of the observed and simulated SFS’s under this demographic model indicated no significant difference in their distributions (two-sided Kolmogorov–Smirnov <em>D</em> = 0.2069, <em>P</em> = 0.564). The fit of the observed data to our simulations suggested that the effective population size of <em>S. mutans</em> has increased 4.8 to 5.5 times since the origin of human agriculture (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-F1"></span><a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="mss278-F1" class="link xref-fig">fig. 1</a><em>c</em>), estimates much larger than those reported for humans (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B18"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B18" data-open="mss278-B18" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Coventry et al. 2010</a>). The likelihood surface of the parameter search was evaluated to assure that it did not fall into local maxima. Models were searched with different initial parameter values and evaluated if they all converged to the same maxima. The likelihood surface in <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-F1"></span><a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="mss278-F1" class="link xref-fig">figure 1</a><em>c</em> was obtained by finding the likelihood value for each combination of parameters in a grid. We estimated the CIs for the parameters by bootstrapping the synonymous data matrix and fitting the model (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S7</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online). </p>                    <a id="90601089" scrollto-destination="90601089"></a>
<div data-id="mss278-f1" data-content-id="mss278-f1" class="fig fig-section js-fig-section" swap-content-for-modal="true"><div class="label fig-label">F<span class="small-caps">ig</span>. 1.</div><div class="graphic-wrap"><img class="content-image" src="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024im_/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/m_mss278f1p.jpeg?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=uWJ8~wnkShRQ0rExmRwS9ydkyoz4kztNAxYnQflW~gg32RzzcivJ05712R8FdOMdef1quvMRWYB64QFv601chelMgWj~GropD0Z3h7556mHxb56OAyK0D32tBpYMePkRN7tn1RpC8Van7UkDa~Vn6MrskMMJCDQ~LbImJYnK3qIp5g-~zy7nzZTMpmpm9ME8MGTa2AKw5xaH-Odxdk0Esgm2uVi2yDccJzo-4vnlBihsQ978um0IHZOvB83o0L78lQg0MmJ9FqQSwuRfW~Mv~dXCFz3Hqo5lGOULfz2AqBcM8Q1uXWTOiA~B2rDNT6YamTUZLLl3vU3Yguy1x5S4hQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA" alt="Demographic history of Streptococcus mutans. (a) Schematic representation of S. mutans population history. The timeline (in years before present) represents the start of the expansion of cariogenic bacteria after the onset of agriculture, calibrated using an experimentally determined mutation rate for bacteria (Drake 1991), concomitant with an in vivo determined generation time for oral flora bacteria (Gibbons 1964) (see Materials and Methods and Supplementary Material online, for details). (b) The observed distribution of number of synonymous SNPs at a given frequency in the sample of 58 isolates (blue) is shown, as well as the expectation under the parameters that generate the best fit demographic model (dark blue). The difference between the two distributions is not significant. The distribution under a standard neutral model with constant population size is shown in light blue (significant KS, P < 0.0001). (c) The bi-dimensional likelihood profile for combination of parameters ν (ratio of current to ancestral population size) in the x axis and the time at the beginning of the demographic expansion (scaled in generations/2Na) in the y axis. The maximum likelihood value is shown as a white dot and the 95% CI is highlighted as a white dotted line. 95% CI estimated from bootstrapped data can be found in supplementary figure S7, Supplementary Material online." data-path-from-xml="mss278f1p.jpeg"/><div class="fig-orig original-slide"><a class="fig-view-orig js-view-large at-figureViewLarge openInAnotherWindow" href="/web/20231227200024/https://academic.oup.com/view-large/figure/90601089/mss278f1p.jpeg" data-path-from-xml="mss278f1p.jpeg" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a><a data-section="90601089" href="/web/20231227200024/https://academic.oup.com/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278f1p.jpeg?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=PaE-tZoKIqtQiCT2qnu-LBTn70PqJkaCT0NMys7SZR2T8T6iuxX-qO4eVTb3zWm~F-BhnTeAGNWyr0f4Lir5wTLQox8VfiHds1nrmC230iv1bUIu6x2k994QOSfQDh0BYgvzAL-VZp51gHnbYtHpsJNENFI4twdHpC7WNWojYpBzSt0tgcWS3wdKS5lRblhWkmG-oY897rFvW669hKIZA~a70rUUQVkXICciAlfx31DiQifqSjNoSRGVceTErC0riJVh1EIPGYvKGuRVNEfxUaJ9EKp9MRZZ-cCg6TQkNcD5Qt6mhqLnl7vsljNhlVs-cuU8k5d~-7bsr0HCLgM3xQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&amp;sec=90601089&amp;ar=1068944&amp;xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&amp;imagename=&amp;siteId=5325" class="download-slide" data-path-from-xml="mss278f1p.jpeg">Download slide</a></div></div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">Demographic history of <em>Streptococcus mutans</em>. (<em>a</em>) Schematic representation of <em>S. mutans</em> population history. The timeline (in years before present) represents the start of the expansion of cariogenic bacteria after the onset of agriculture, calibrated using an experimentally determined mutation rate for bacteria (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B26"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B26" data-open="mss278-B26" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Drake 1991</a>), concomitant with an in vivo determined generation time for oral flora bacteria (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B36"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B36" data-open="mss278-B36" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Gibbons 1964</a>) (see Materials and Methods and <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online, for details). (<em>b</em>) The observed distribution of number of synonymous SNPs at a given frequency in the sample of 58 isolates (blue) is shown, as well as the expectation under the parameters that generate the best fit demographic model (dark blue). The difference between the two distributions is not significant. The distribution under a standard neutral model with constant population size is shown in light blue (significant KS, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.0001). (<em>c</em>) The bi-dimensional likelihood profile for combination of parameters <em>ν</em> (ratio of current to ancestral population size) in the <em>x</em> axis and the time at the beginning of the demographic expansion (scaled in generations/2Na) in the <em>y</em> axis. The maximum likelihood value is shown as a white dot and the 95% CI is highlighted as a white dotted line. 95% CI estimated from bootstrapped data can be found in <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary figure S7</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online.</p></div></div>                    <a id="90601090" scrollto-destination="90601090"></a>

                        graphic-wrap
