The third inner circle, displays the unique core genes, present in S. mutans only, colored by the metabolic functions in which they are involved. The most inner circles present the unique genes shown to be upregulated or downregulated by the impacts coincident with the diet change of humans after the origin of agriculture: starch and sucrose metabolism and low environmental pH. (b) Putative origin of laterally transferred unique core genes in S. mutans.." data-path-from-xml="mss278f4p.jpeg"/><div class="fig-orig original-slide"><a class="fig-view-orig js-view-large at-figureViewLarge openInAnotherWindow" href="/web/20231227200024/https://academic.oup.com/view-large/figure/90601098/mss278f4p.jpeg" data-path-from-xml="mss278f4p.jpeg" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a><a data-section="90601098" href="/web/20231227200024/https://academic.oup.com/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278f4p.jpeg?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=A~hTYZXArmeszJqBU1eoep5F6lxFqK3iSRudlgHJ6wVqpVyBTaCWJlbdBzxCN28Os6eH09pF~mfoRVUd7nVyD5okQZSH44NN4sqRYEiTfIplXpmaP6ekBYvVC0MX1cxsdNUQvChhgPxVON3HICpPseyzQDywzHyq14exG5j43XMYhI8-aKA00AhLO~3PfXaBxh-yENoqu6yUn1HqqrsErGH7zrasJwvypmnn5b6f4iJK6fg8GOqL2DTKhWiRCxQiSkVo8fLbRUf8YwdGZZqiowHeP5d1HsG8MY1aVmLEeY1YWqfvdpHVG06SBFX8eQBcWabhBK9TDAgZXnpqPiq0kQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&amp;sec=90601098&amp;ar=1068944&amp;xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&amp;imagename=&amp;siteId=5325" class="download-slide" data-path-from-xml="mss278f4p.jpeg">Download slide</a></div></div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">Genome map of <em>Streptococcus mutans</em>. (<em>a</em>) Representation of the forward coding (light blue) and reverse coding (light red) genes comprising the core genome of <em>S. mutans</em>. The third inner circle, displays the unique core genes, present in <em>S. mutans</em> only, colored by the metabolic functions in which they are involved. The most inner circles present the unique genes shown to be upregulated or downregulated by the impacts coincident with the diet change of humans after the origin of agriculture: starch and sucrose metabolism and low environmental pH. (<em>b</em>) Putative origin of laterally transferred unique core genes in <em>S. mutans.</em>.</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="90601099" id="90601099" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Discussion</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">The genomes of different strains of <em>S. mutans</em>, like those of many other species of <em>Streptococcus</em>, are highly dynamic and their overall gene composition differs markedly from one isolate to another because of lateral gene transfer (LGT). However, as with other bacteria, this difference in gene content involves only a portion of the genome, generally referred to as the dispensable component, in contrast to an alternative set of genes common to all strains, known as the core genome. Together these two components comprise the pan-genome of the species (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B79"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B79" data-open="mss278-B79" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Tettelin et al. 2005</a>, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B80"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B80" data-open="mss278-B80" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2008</a>). The core genome is a clearly identifiable component of <em>Streptococcus</em> species, as well as species from other genera, and indeed may represent that set of genes which can best define bacterial species (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B50"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B50" data-open="mss278-B50" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Lan and Reeves 2000</a>, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B51"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B51" data-open="mss278-B51" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2001</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B80"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B80" data-open="mss278-B80" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Tettelin et al. 2008</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B52"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B52" data-open="mss278-B52" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Lapierre and Gogarten 2009</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B53"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B53" data-open="mss278-B53" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Lefebure et al. 2010</a>). Our analyses suggest that the core genome of the species is readily identifiable and consists of 1,430 genes. The complete gene repertoire of the dispensable component of the genome is much harder to capture. Nevertheless, after examining the accumulation curve of the pan-genome in <em>S. mutans</em> (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S3</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online), we suggest that by sampling 58 genomes in the population we are reaching a point at which there is no appreciable increase in the number of new genes as more genomes are included in the analysis.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="90601101" id="90601101" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Demographic History</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">Our estimates for the timing of the demographic expansion in <em>S. mutans</em> coincide with the origin of human agriculture. It has been hypothesized that many infectious diseases could only originate and be maintained after humankind developed agriculture (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B33"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B33" data-open="mss278-B33" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Fiennes 1978</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B24"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B24" data-open="mss278-B24" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Dobson and Carper 1996</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B21"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B21" data-open="mss278-B21" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Diamond 2002</a>). Numerous studies in physical anthropology have shown an increased prevalence of dental caries in human remains from post-agricultural societies (5–50%) when compared with remains of Mesolithic hunter–gatherers (0–2%) (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B17"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B17" data-open="mss278-B17" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Cohen and Armelagos 1984</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B34"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B34" data-open="mss278-B34" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Formicola 1987</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B57"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B57" data-open="mss278-B57" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Lukacs 1992</a>). This pattern has been attributed to changes in diet and the consequent increase in consumption of carbohydrates in human populations after the development of starchy crops, leading to the establishment of infectious agents causing dental caries (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B17"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B17" data-open="mss278-B17" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Cohen and Armelagos 1984</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B57"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B57" data-open="mss278-B57" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Lukacs 1992</a>). Our timing and expansion estimates are consistent with <em>S. mutans</em> as a possible etiological agent of human dental caries; however, it is not possible to rule out other oral bacteria species as also contributing to the development of this disease.</p><p class="chapter-para">We have attempted to be thorough in our analysis, by including not only rigorous demonstration that <em>S. mutans</em> undergoes recombination but also that the methods of analysis are appropriate for the limited conditions of a small genome size, and other constraints imposed by the biology of this organism. Nevertheless, we have not explored all alternative possibilities that could give rise to patterns of variation like those observed in this data set. We have provided a parsimonious explanation for the pattern of variation found in the synonymous substitutions along the core genome; however, the biology of bacterial species is such that there are potential alternative conditions that could give rise to similar patterns. For instance, it is widely appreciated that selection can impact the population dynamics of clonal organisms like bacteria (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B27"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B27" data-open="mss278-B27" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Dykhuizen 1990a</a>, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B28"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B28" data-open="mss278-B28" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">1990b</a>, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B29"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B29" data-open="mss278-B29" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">1993</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B40"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B40" data-open="mss278-B40" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Guttman and Dykhuizen 1994</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B12"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B12" data-open="mss278-B12" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Buckee et al. 2008</a>). Although it is possible that a scenario of multiple instances of selection and linkage, because of linkage to replacement sites under selection (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B11"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B11" data-open="mss278-B11" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Braverman et al. 1995</a>), could explain the excess of singletons in the distribution of synonymous changes, it is difficult to envision a selection scenario, with specific selective pressures, that would explain our observations. Another potential scenario is the existence of different regimes of mutation in time. In laboratory experiments, it has been shown how clones with greater mutation rates (mutators) can facilitate adaptation to new environmental conditions (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B76"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B76" data-open="mss278-B76" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Sniegowski, et al. 1997</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B73"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B73" data-open="mss278-B73" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Shaver, et al. 2002</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B35"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B35" data-open="mss278-B35" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Gerrish et al. 2007</a>). These mutators also have the effect of changing the overall mutation rate in the population, potentially affecting the pattern of observed variation, giving rise to an observed excess of singletons, via a combination of negative selection on replacement changes and variation of the rates at which new synonymous and replacement changes are being generated. Unfortunately, there is no information about the prevalence of mutator strains in natural populations (carrier or clinical) of <em>S. mutans</em>, or in experiments performed with laboratory strains. Our analyses of the population genetic structure of the data, consistent with results from previous work (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B23"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B23" data-open="mss278-B23" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Do et al. 2010</a>), suggest that there is no geographic differentiation. We believe that our simulations under the inferred demographic scenario, which exclude selection, provide a sufficiently parsimonious explanation in agreement with the observed data, to explain the genome wide accumulation of synonymous variation in <em>S. mutans</em>. It has been suggested that <em>S. mutans</em> genotypes are maternally transmitted (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B3"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B3" data-open="mss278-B3" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Alaluusua et al. 1996</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B31"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B31" data-open="mss278-B31" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Emanuelsson et al. 1998</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B32"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B32" data-open="mss278-B32" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Emanuelsson and Thornqvist 2000</a>), and this could act to produce a strong pattern of geographic differentiation of the isolates over time. Our results showing a lack of geographic structure are not consistent with such a scenario. Recent work by <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B49"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B49" data-open="mss278-B49" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Klein et al. (2004)</a> suggests a high turnover rate of lineages in the mouth of children after colonization from the mother, which would be consistent with our observations.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="90601104" id="90601104" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Adaptation in the Postagricultural Era</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">The distribution of replacement variants in the <em>S. mutans</em> population is consistent with strong purifying selection acting genome-wide, and it raises the question of what are the features of molecular adaptation that underlie <em>S. mutans</em> successful colonization of, and proliferation in, the human host more than 10,000 years ago. To adapt to the new niche of the “post-agricultural” human mouth, <em>S. mutans</em> faced several challenges. Among them, <em>S. mutans</em> needed to develop, or increase, efficiency in the metabolism of new sugars, successfully compete with bacterial species already present in the mouth of humans, develop defenses against increased oxidative stress, and resist the acidic by-products of its own new efficient carbohydrate metabolism (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B47"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B47" data-open="mss278-B47" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Jacobson et al. 1989</a>). Or results suggest that only a handful of genes (14 genes) present a pattern that deviates from the neutral expectation. On the other hand, analysis of the function of the unique core genes suggests that a large fraction of these genes are involved in sugar metabolism and pH resistance. The absence of these putative adaptive genes in other species of the Mutans taxonomic group (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B78"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B78" data-open="mss278-B78" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Tapp et al. 2003</a>) suggests their acquisition via LGT to the <em>S. mutans</em> lineage. Consistent with this hypothesis, these proteins tend to be similar to those arising from a wide variety of bacterial species including other oral flora bacteria, as well as taxa which produce lactic acid (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-F4"></span><a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="mss278-F4" class="link xref-fig">fig. 4</a><em>b</em>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary table S2</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online), and many of them appear to be involved in carbohydrate metabolism. As an example, the gene SMU.438 (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S11</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online) is closely related to a hypothetical protein present in <em>Lactococcus lactis,</em> which includes domains corresponding to an activator of 2-hydroxyglutaryl-CoA dehydratase. This protein includes three domains identified in <em>Lactobacillus:</em> FGGY family of carbohydrate kinases, N-terminal domain (2), and a CoA-substrate-specific enzyme activase, suggesting that this protein could be involved in carbohydrate metabolism (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B48"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B48" data-open="mss278-B48" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Klees et al. 1992</a>). The protein SMU.1410 (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S12</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online) is very similar to a putative reductase of <em>S. </em><em>gallolyticus</em> (formerly known as <em>S. </em><em>bovis). S. gallolyticus</em> is found in the gastrointestinal tract of ruminants and humans, and has been linked to endocarditis in humans (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B43"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B43" data-open="mss278-B43" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Hoen et al. 2005</a>). When ruminants consume a diet high in sugars, the fermentable carbohydrates allow for the growth of <em>S. bovis</em> (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B5"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B5" data-open="mss278-B5" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Asanuma and Hino 2002</a>). This organism also exhibits lactic acid fermentation, reducing the pH of the rumen leading to ruminal acidosis (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B5"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B5" data-open="mss278-B5" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Asanuma and Hino 2002</a>). Reductases are also involved as ion pumps in electron transport chains. We suggest that this putative reductase is involved in resistance to acidic conditions in <em>S. mutans</em> and was obtained via a LGT event from <em>S. gallolyticus,</em> or some very closely related taxon. The protein SMU.1561 has been shown to be up-regulated in <em>S. mutans</em> in acid conditions (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B37"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B37" data-open="mss278-B37" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Gong et al. 2009</a>). Our phylogenetic analysis indicates that SMU.1561 (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S13</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online) is closely related to the product of the <em>trkA</em> gene from <em>Ethanoligenens harbinense,</em> which is another bacteria forming part of the oral flora of humans (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B1"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B1" data-open="mss278-B1" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Aas et al. 2005</a>). The <em>trkA</em> gene encodes a protein involved in the transport of ions across the membrane (Anion permease ArsB/NhaD), which is consistent with the fact that it is upregulated in acidic conditions (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B37"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B37" data-open="mss278-B37" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Gong et al. 2009</a>). An alternative explanation, to a history of LGT for these unique core genes, is that they arose through vertical descent from one of these close relatives of <em>S. mutans</em>, but the genes are not part of the core genome of these other taxa and instead are present in their dispensable genomes, and we simply have not yet sampled them in a single genome sequence. We have identified elsewhere (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B53"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B53" data-open="mss278-B53" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Lefebure et al. 2010</a>) that core genes in one bacterial species can have their origins in the dispensable genome of closely related bacteria. Whatever their precise evolutionary history, these genes may be important loci in defining the caries-associated phenotype of <em>S. mutans</em> and its adaptation to the human mouth environment.</p><p class="chapter-para">Although low pH has been considered a primary ecological determinant influencing oral biofilm ecology, oxygen is also a critical factor (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B94"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B94" data-open="mss278-B94" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Marquis 1995</a>), and it appears to be much better tolerated by commensal streptococci and other members of the normal microbiota than by <em>S. mutans</em> (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B94"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B94" data-open="mss278-B94" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Marquis 1995</a>). In fact, exposure to oxygen strongly inhibits biofilm formation by <em>S. mutans</em> and alters the transcriptome and metabolism in a way that renders it less cariogenic (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B88"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B88" data-open="mss278-B88" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Ahn and Burne 2007</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B87"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B87" data-open="mss278-B87" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Ahn et al. 2009</a>). Thus, <em>S. mutans</em> likely does not compete well in conditions of high redox or oxygen tension. Recently, hydrogen peroxide production by health-associated streptococci, such as <em>S. </em><em>gordonii</em>, has been demonstrated to strongly inhibit <em>S. mutans</em> in mixed culture (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B92"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B92" data-open="mss278-B92" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Kreth et al. 2008</a>). In addition to genes involved in acid tolerance, the unique core genes of <em>S. mutans</em> contain a high proportion of small peptides and gene products that could potentially be involved in oxidative stress tolerance (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary table S3</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online). For example, the secreted peptide encoded by SMU.1131c (ciaX) has been shown to modulate signal transduction in the CiaRH two-component system to regulate acid and oxidative stress tolerance and genetic competence (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B42"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B42" data-open="mss278-B42" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">He et al. 2008</a>). A variety of other peptides encoded in the unique core genome are in operons with transporters (e.g., SMU.18, SMU.185, and SMU.390), with transcriptional regulators that control gene expression in response to stressors (e.g., the MarR-type regulators associated with SMU.390, SMU.438, SMU.444, and SMU.631), or with proteins that may influence oxidative stress tolerance (e.g., SMU.545 is genetically linked to the <em>dpr</em> gene that encodes a protein necessary for oxidative stress resistance [<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary table S3</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online]). Of note, a distinct MarR transcriptional regulator of <em>S. mutans</em> (SMU.925) was recently demonstrated to regulate the expression of two ABC-exporters to link (p)ppGpp metabolism, genetic competence and oxidative stress tolerance, possibly through a peptide-based sensing system (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B72"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B72" data-open="mss278-B72" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Seaton et al. 2011</a>). SMU.1047c is a small peptide genetically linked to the <em>relQ</em> operon, which encodes the RelQ (p)ppGpp synthase and three other gene products contributing to oxidative stress tolerance (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B91"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B91" data-open="mss278-B91" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Kim et al. 2012</a>). Smu.185 is part of the <em>slo</em> operon, which regulates manganous ion internalization, oxidative stress tolerance, and expression of a variety of genes including superoxide dismutate. Likewise, SMU.1645 is predicted to mediate tellurite resistance, which is generally associated with thiol-mediated reduction of tellurite, whereas SMU.642 is cotranscribed in a complex operon with a predicted oxidoreductase, competence-associated peptide and peptidase; possibly linking oxidative stress and competence similar to the SMU.925 operon (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B72"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B72" data-open="mss278-B72" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Seaton et al. 2011</a>). Thus, while low pH provides strong selective pressure for aciduric species, during fermentable carbohydrate consumption and caries initiation and progression, oxygen may be an equally important environmental factor influencing the composition, biochemistry, and pathogenic potential of oral biofilms (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B86"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B86" data-open="mss278-B86" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Abbe et al. 1991</a>); and may be the underlying explanation for the relative prevalence of proteins with roles in oxidative stress carried in the unique core genome of <em>S. mutans</em> (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary table S3</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online).</p><p class="chapter-para"><em>S. mutans</em> is also capable of mounting a substantial defense against commensal streptococci. In particular, strains of <em>S. mutans</em> produce a variety of lantibiotic and nonlantiobiotic bacteriocins that can kill related organisms (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B90"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B90" data-open="mss278-B90" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Balakrishnan et al. 2002</a>). Peptide-based quorum-sensing systems, including the ComC competence cascade, multiple two-component systems, density-dependent signaling complexes and global regulatory systems all cooperate to influence the production of bacteriocin-like molecules (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B95"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B95" data-open="mss278-B95" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Martin et al. 2006</a>). The <em>hdrR</em> gene (SMU.1854) of the <em>S. mutans</em> unique core genome encodes a DNA binding protein that regulates bacteriocin production in a density-dependent fashion through its interactions with the HdrM membrane protein (SMU.1855) to control genetic competence and bacteriocin production (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B63"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B63" data-open="mss278-B63" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Merritt et al. 2007</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B67"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B67" data-open="mss278-B67" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Okinaga et al. 2010</a>). Interestingly, exposure to air uniformly activates the bacteriocin pathways and endogenous bacteriocin immunity systems, probably as a defense mechanism against competing organisms in immature, comparatively aerobic dental biofilms (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B87"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B87" data-open="mss278-B87" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Ahn et al. 2009</a>). Therefore, it is significant that the unique core genes of <em>S. mutans</em> contain a higher proportion of small peptides and gene products (smaller than 100 amino acids) than the core genome as a whole (approximately 6:1 ratio) that could potentially be involved in signaling and/or gene regulation (binomial test comparison with core genome, <em>P</em> = 1.23e−10; <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary table S5</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="90601108" id="90601108" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Conclusion</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">Taken collectively, our findings suggest that the <em>S. mutans</em> unique core genes may represent important pathogen-specific factors that could be targeted with species-specific therapeutics that might decrease the competitive fitness of <em>S. mutans</em> without interfering with the propagation of health-associated commensal organisms. This study also suggests that one of the innovations that formed the basis of civilization may have precipitated a long-term association with an important human pathogen, highlighting the interconnections that exist between our sociocultural and biological evolution.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="90601110" id="90601110" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Materials and Methods</h2>
                    <h3 scrollto-destination="90601111" id="90601111" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">DNA Sequencing and Ortholog Recovery</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">A total of 57 strains of <em>S. mutans</em> were selected representing different MLST and countries of origin: Brazil (<em>n</em> = 16), UK (<em>n</em> = 29), Iceland (<em>n</em> = 5), Hong Kong (<em>n</em> = 2), South Africa (<em>n</em> = 2), Turkey (<em>n</em> = 2), and USA (<em>n</em> = 1) (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary table S4</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online). Single end sequencing was performed using the Illumina GA2 sequencer, with one lane per strain. The sequence read lengths averaged 36–46 bp. This ensured high coverage (∼70×) of the ∼2 MB genome of <em>S. mutans</em>. Sequence reads were aligned to the <em>S. mutans</em> UA159 and <em>S. mutans</em> NN2025 complete genomes, respectively, using MAQ (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B93"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B93" data-open="mss278-B93" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Li, Ruan, et al. 2008</a>), with appropriate mapping quality and coverage filters applied to capture the sequence information. Consensus alignments generated from the MAQ mapping were employed to extract, realign, and perform SNP calls in core genome genes; that is, genes for which all strains could be mapped to the reference. De novo assemblies were performed using Velvet (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B83"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B83" data-open="mss278-B83" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Zerbino and Birney 2008</a>). Several hash lengths (from 21 to 31) and coverage cut-offs (0 to 80) were used, and the best assembly per strain was selected based on a combination of the N50 (which refers to a weighted median with 50% of the entire assembly contained in contigs equal to or greater than this value), the total assembly and the longest contig size. A schematic diagram for the mapping/assembly pipeline is included in <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary figure S14</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online. Assembled genomes were annotated using the NCBI Prokaryotic Genomes Automatic Annotation Pipeline (PGAAP). This pipeline combines HMM-based predictions with sequence similarity approaches to generate comparisons of the query genes or coding sequences against the gene products available in nonredundant protein databases: Entrez Protein Clusters, the Conserved Domain Database and the COGs (Clusters of Orthologous Groups). The algorithms employed in the pipeline are those implemented in GeneMark and Glimmer (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B9"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B9" data-open="mss278-B9" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Borodovsky and Mcininch 1993</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B58"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B58" data-open="mss278-B58" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Lukashin and Borodovsky 1998</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B20"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B20" data-open="mss278-B20" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Delcher et al. 1999</a>).</p><p class="chapter-para">To identify the orthologous sequences present in <em>S. ratti</em>, the putative sister group to <em>S. mutans</em> (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B78"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B78" data-open="mss278-B78" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Tapp et al. 2003</a>), we sequenced the genome of one isolate of <em>S. ratti</em> using 454 GS-FLX with paired end, followed by partial gap closure with PCR. The sequence of this isolate was assembled using Newbler and annotated using PGAAP (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary fig. S15</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online). The rationale for sequencing <em>S. ratti</em> and identifying <em>S. ratii</em>/<em>S. mutans</em> orthologs was 2-fold: 1) identify orthologous genes that could be used as outgroups in a selection analysis that involved comparison of polymorphism and divergence data; and 2) identify core genes present in <em>S. mutans</em>, but not the most closely related species—that is, the putative unique core genome of <em>S. mutans</em>. Orthologs were determined by performing an all-versus-all BLASTP search combined with clustering using OrthoMCL2 (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B55"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B55" data-open="mss278-B55" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Li et al. 2003</a>), and included all the <em>S. mutans</em> de novo assembled genomes and the genome sequence for the sister group <em>S. ratti</em>. The history of the <em>S. mutans</em> unique genes could involve losses along the <em>S. ratti</em> lineage or gains via LGT along the <em>S. mutans</em> lineage. Core genes present in <em>S. mutans</em> but absent in other closely related mutans group streptococci, in addition to the sister group <em>S. ratti</em>, represent genes most likely to have been acquired by LGT. To identify such genes, we obtained draft genome sequences (454 GS-FLX, paired end) of two other mutans group streptococci, closely related to <em>S. mutans</em>: <em>S. </em><em>macacae</em><em>,</em> and <em>S. </em><em>criceti</em> (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B78"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B78" data-open="mss278-B78" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Tapp et al. 2003</a>). The sequences of <em>S. criceti</em> and <em>S. macacae</em> were assembled with Newbler and annotated using PGAAP. Once the protein clusters were identified, we performed an all versus all BLAST comparison of the putatively unique <em>S. mutans</em> genes with the annotated genes of these additional two species, using OrthoMCL2 (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B55"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B55" data-open="mss278-B55" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Li, Stoeckert, et al. 2003</a>). A conservative similarity threshold of 50% was set as a measure of whether a gene in <em>S. mutans</em> had an ortholog in either of the other two more divergent species. The presence or absence of the genes putatively unique to <em>S. mutans</em>, in these other members of the mutans group, helped us distinguish which genes were lost in the <em>S. ratti</em> lineage and which ones were gained by <em>S. mutans</em>. Finally, additional BLAST searches against existing databases (NCBI nr), followed by maximum likelihood phylogenetic analysis of the aligned sequences, were employed to evaluate the evolutionary history and putative donor species of the unique genes identified as gained on the <em>S. mutans</em> lineage. A list of the additional streptococci genomes, included in this analysis (available at NCBI at the time), is provided in <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary table S5</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online.</p><p class="chapter-para">Genome sequence data for 57 strains of <em>S. mutans</em> and single strains each of <em>S. ratti</em> (FA-1), <em>S. criceti</em> (HS-6), and <em>S. macacae</em> (NCTC 11558) have been deposited in GenBank under the following accession numbers: <em>S. ratti</em>: AJTZ01000000; <em>S. criceti</em>: AEUV01000016.1; <em>S. macacae</em>: AEUW01000012.1. Accessions for the <em>S. mutans</em> isolates can be found in <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">supplementary table S4</a></span>, <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="90601115" id="90601115" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">SNP Calling</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">The 1,430 genes constituting the core genome of <em>S. mutans</em>, were realigned at the protein level to ensure that the alignments were in frame. Synonymous and replacement changes (and potential sites) were estimated following an “in house” pipeline coupled to the d<em>N</em>d<em>S</em> routine implemented in the Libsequence suit (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B81"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B81" data-open="mss278-B81" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Thornton 2003</a>). Because of the deep coverage of our data (&gt;70×), we were confident in the call of rare variants (singletons) and no further sophisticated methods were employed for their identification. Each gene alignment was analyzed with the Polydnds program of the Libsequence suite developed by Thornton to estimate the number of synonymous and nonsynonymous unambiguous changes in the alignment. These data were used in three ways: 1) genome-wide synonymous and nonsynonymous changes were employed to estimate the SFS for the synonymous and nonsynonymous changes respectively, as well as for the PCA to assess structure; 2) the synonymous and nonsynonymous changes per gene, with the inclusion of the outgroup <em>S. ratti</em> were used in selection analyses; 3). the number of synonymous and replacement positions for each gene served as denominators in assessing the polymorphism per site, as well as in estimating the mutation parameter <em>θ</em> (2Neu in haploid organisms) for the demographic and selection analyses.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="90601117" id="90601117" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Estimation of Recombination</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">Recombination, or more formally gene conversion, was estimated on the core genome alignment of the full data set using Sawyer’s algorithm as implemented in GeneConv (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B70"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B70" data-open="mss278-B70" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Sawyer 1989</a>), keeping only those tracts that were judged significant after Bonferroni correction for multiple comparisons, in a manner similar to which it was implemented in <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B54"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B54" data-open="mss278-B54" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Leopold et al. (2009)</a>. This algorithm estimates the distribution of the length of identical sequences between any two pairs, considering the distribution of variant sites in the multiple sequence alignment. Using a permutation-based test, any identical fragment between pairs of sequences that is unusually long, as expected from the estimated distribution, is considered to be the result of a gene conversion (recombination) event. In addition to these analyses, we constructed three sets, each comprised of 600 genomic regions of 500-bp long. These sets were used to estimate the relative contribution of recombination to mutation as proposed by <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B22"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B22" data-open="mss278-B22" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Didelot and Falush (2007)</a>, and implemented in the program ClonalFrame. The conditions of the run included 100,000 generations of burnin, 100,000 generations for each chain to run, and step sizes for the sampling of 100 generations.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="90601119" id="90601119" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Demographic and Selection Analyses</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">PCA (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B68"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B68" data-open="mss278-B68" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Patterson et al. 2006</a>) of synonymous SNPs with frequencies greater than 5%, was performed using the R project for Statistical Computing (<a class="link link-uri openInAnotherWindow" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/http://www.r-project.org/" target="_blank">http://www.r-project.org/</a>, last accessed December 15, 2012). Rare variants were not included, as these do not contribute to distinguishing relatedness among individuals in putative subpopulations. The frequency distribution of variants, or SFS, was calculated for synonymous and replacement changes independently in R. Demographic parameters for different competing models were estimated from the SFS of synonymous changes using a diffusion-based approximation implemented in the program <em>δ</em>a<em>δ</em>i (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B39"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B39" data-open="mss278-B39" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Gutenkunst et al. 2009</a>) in a maximum likelihood framework. The selection of the best-fit model was done using the Akaike Information Criteria. Changes in effective population size and time since the change in demographics are estimated in 2Neu and 2Ne scaled parameters, respectively. To convert these values to actual population sizes (expressed in individuals) and time (in years) we assumed a mutation rate estimated experimentally for bacteria between 4.08e−10 and 6.93e−10 subs/site/generation (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B26"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B26" data-open="mss278-B26" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Drake 1991</a>; <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B66"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B66" data-open="mss278-B66" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Ochman 2003</a>), corresponding to 0.112–0.379 subst/genome/year (given that there are 374,571 synonymous sites along the genome), under the conservative assumption of a generation time of two divisions per day, as estimated for oral flora in vivo (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B36"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B36" data-open="mss278-B36" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Gibbons 1964</a>). This mutation rate is consistent with experimentally determined mutation rates, estimated via fluctuation tests, in wild type clones of another member of the genus, <em>S. </em><em>pyogenes</em>, at approximately 5.3 × 10e−10 mutations/generation (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B71"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B71" data-open="mss278-B71" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Scott et al. 2008</a>). CIs of the parameters were estimated by maximum likelihood fitting of 500 bootstrapped data sets (details in <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20231227200024/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mbe/30/4/10.1093_molbev_mss278/2/mss278_Supplementary_Data.zip?Expires=1706094324&amp;Signature=q-9ypMXmrfAJvE7V-MZQLJO7ttAzgHJVHCfNvAE0rveR6hLlwftoPuK37ahtH2E4E69KobbC4YiXfS6os4UTS8iLUw38AxP4uTMYpYy1ZvC3IkVkoXVVnovPRUo3i-wW0E5urFieRvtQK4xgAiYZxvzscXmjqOnYQzBRC3eRJDXyNHKHTcEfgwFPDBr6soxfiCin1EOnR~8W1PtgYwlCylIeaQRa2O-ZFVEJfZit54njdUewITLJyVfFNYO~AV4yMsUpq2jtG0oHLmcLF1~vTcxloS307J0UhPUX~d9BQf3KWV716Ra6AEIKrn3RvrJAbnaHTQhvM4MRkVcsbl-Ehg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Material</a></span> online). We also performed simulations under the estimated demographics and recombination rates, and performed estimations of the parameters in the simulated data sets using <em>δ</em>a<em>δ</em>i.</p><p class="chapter-para">Genome wide selection analyses were performed on the replacement SFS by a similar diffusion-based approximation as the one employed for the demographic analysis. The impact of selection on the distribution of replacement mutations genome-wide was evaluated considering models that treat selection as a point mass effect or as a distribution of selective effects. Of these, three main models (one, two, or three categories of sites under selection with constant selection coefficients) were evaluated by maximum likelihood and over bootstrapped data sets, in the program PrFreq (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B10"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B10" data-open="mss278-B10" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Boyko et al. 2008</a>). Again, the best model was selected using the Akaike Information Criteria. We also performed a standard MK test (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B62"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B62" data-open="mss278-B62" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">McDonald and Kreitman 1991</a>), and an extension of the MK test approach based on a Bayesian Loglinear model, implemented in SnIPRE (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-mss278-B89"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="mss278-B89" data-open="mss278-B89" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">Eilertson et al. 2012</a>), to compare the polymorphism and divergent changes in synonymous and replacement sites in the genes for which an orthologous sequence could be identified in <em>S. ratti</em> (82% of the core genes in <em>S. mutans</em>). The model implemented in SnIPRE is a Bayesian generalization of the log-linear model underlying the MK test that makes use of both genome-wide and gene-specific effects to model the variability among categories of mutations, resulting in increased power to detect the effects of selection on a gene-by-gene basis. It is nonparametric in the sense that it makes no assumptions (and does not require estimation) of parameters such as mutation rate and species divergence time to identify genes under selection.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="90601122" id="90601122" class="backacknowledgements-title js-splitscreen-backacknowledgements-title">Acknowledgments</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">Scott Durkin of JCVI provided some comparative genomic analysis on early drafts of the <em>S</em>. <em>criceti</em> and <em>S</em>. <em>macacae</em> genome sequences. This work was supported by the National Institute of Allergy and Infectious Disease, US National Institutes of Health (grant number AI073368 to M.J.S.).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="90601124" id="90601124" class="backreferences-title js-splitscreen-backreferences-title">References</h2>

data-id="mss278-B87"><div class="citation element-citation"><span class="person-group"><div class="name"><div class="surname">Ahn</div> <div class="given-names">SJ</div></div>,  <div class="name"><div
